<div id="review_637915422" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/637915422" />
    <a title="Melanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10890208-melanie"><img alt="Melanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413275441p2/10890208.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/637915422?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melanie" href="/user/show/10890208-melanie">Melanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780857532503" href="/book/show/13608989-the-iron-trial">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=could-have-been-so-much-better">could-have-been-so-much-better</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=bleh">bleh</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=paranormal">paranormal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=arc">arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer637915422" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9272278985824495746">See more reviews at <a href='http://yamidnightreads.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>YA Midnight Reads</a><br/><br/><b>1.5 stars</b><br/><br/>When reading a book, one expects the following: 1) an original storyline and 2) one that will not make you fall asleep one chapter in. The Iron Trial, despite being co-written by two totally capable authors, just didn’t make 1) or 2) happen.<br/><br/><strong>Harry Potter, anyone? I bet we all saw this one coming, regardless whether you’ve read the book yet or not.</strong> It’s about tweens, it’s got magic, it’s got 3 freaking kids on the front cover and in the background, the</span>
  <span id="freeText9272278985824495746" style="display:none">See more reviews at <a href="http://yamidnightreads.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">YA Midnight Reads</a><br /><br /><b>1.5 stars</b><br /><br />When reading a book, one expects the following: 1) an original storyline and 2) one that will not make you fall asleep one chapter in. The Iron Trial, despite being co-written by two totally capable authors, just didn’t make 1) or 2) happen.<br /><br /><strong>Harry Potter, anyone? I bet we all saw this one coming, regardless whether you’ve read the book yet or not.</strong> It’s about tweens, it’s got magic, it’s got 3 freaking kids on the front cover and in the background, there is a creepy looking thing that could draw parallels with good ole’ Voldermort. Personally, I wanted to hold all pre-judgement aside and read it seeing as it’s written by Cassie Clare and Holly Black for goodness sake. Unfortunately, these parallels only became more prominent and infuriatingly annoying after reading the book.<strong> The atmosphere was practically singing Hogwarts, but only not as well executed.</strong> I expected really imaginative and big things from this pair, however, it just wasn’t the case here.<br /><br />The book starts with Callum sitting a series of tests to see if he can gain entrance to the Magisterium where he can learn magic from mages. His father is completely against the Magisterium after his wife died at their expense, and he has been forcing Callum to fail The Iron Trial on purpose so no mage will take him up as an apprentice. Callum does obey his father, though strangely, a mage chooses him anyway, and Callum has no choice but to go to the Magisterium.<br /><br /><strong>Less ideal, were the characters.</strong> There’s plenty of diversity in terms of personality and cultural backgrounds, yet they lack substance. I felt no real grasp on the characters at all—they lacked that emotional bond and<strong> didn’t spring out of the page at all.</strong> Not even Callum, the main character. These are simply not characters that I would root for; <strong>they lacked depth and shape</strong> and even though this is an MG novel, and I know MG characters can be crafted well—<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13129136-the-year-of-shadows?ac=1" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Year of Shadows by Claire Legrand</a> being one notable example. (To be honest, I’ve already forgotten every other characters’ names aside from Callum.)<br /><br />Another thing that this first instalment lacked was the idea of keeping the reader actually interested and engaged. <strong>No jokes, I fell asleep after reading chapter one.</strong> And that never happens to me. But you can’t blame me though, the first paragraph was a boring, character profile info-dump. If chapter one couldn’t keep me interested, then you can assume what I did for about the last half of the book. Yep, I skimmed it. This felt very much like a set up for a series because we were just getting a lot of irrelevant info-dumps and monotonous and completely unnecessary events happening. Not that that is what’s supposed to happen in a first instalment.<br /><br /><strong>I cannot recommend this book for it has disappointed me deeply.</strong> I highly doubt that I’ll bother continuing with this series as the characters and storyline failed to engage me entirely.<br /><p>
  <em>~Thank you Random House Australia for sending me this copy!~</em>
</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="9272278985824495746" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andre" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andre" href="/user/show/6431131-andre">Andre</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780857532503" href="/book/show/13608989-the-iron-trial">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431131-andre?shelf=don-t-read-it">don-t-read-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431131-andre?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer969040968" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9702526566029655651">So, a while ago I came across the freely available excerpt for this book, which has 21 pages in PDF format, 15 pages of story and contained the prologue and the first chapter. So I figured, let's see whether it can catch my interest. And did it? Not in the least. As a matter of fact you would need to give me a damn good reason to ever actually read it because I am sure as hell not going to waste any more time on this book than absolutely necessary.<br/>Sure considered who one of the authors is I coul</span>
  <span id="freeText9702526566029655651" style="display:none">So, a while ago I came across the freely available excerpt for this book, which has 21 pages in PDF format, 15 pages of story and contained the prologue and the first chapter. So I figured, let's see whether it can catch my interest. And did it? Not in the least. As a matter of fact you would need to give me a damn good reason to ever actually read it because I am sure as hell not going to waste any more time on this book than absolutely necessary.<br />Sure considered who one of the authors is I could have seen a lot worse writing, but maybe that is rather due to the influence of the second author rather than the first one. Because you see, while the writing leaves a lot to be desired and I still constantly asked myself who writes like that and in some cases what this and that is even supposed to mean, most of that had its weirdness in context<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[, like when we are told that cave in the glacier was for sick, old and wounded but yet there is not a single description of a bed or anything <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, rather than being weird within itself because the sentence just makes no plain sense.<br />And while the writing is bad in my eyes and the cover also leaves a lot to be desired<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[(it looks pretty bland, not really saying anything. The blond guy is of course in the center and especially the brunette, possibly brown-skinned girl looks not only timid, child-like and hunched but it looks as though her right (from my perspective) eye is trailing in a different direction than her left) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> what is the excerpt's biggest flaw is its utmost stupidity in plot and writing.<br />And I wish it would start with the prologue, but nope, the first text I got in the excerpt was some pretentious comment by the authors telling me, an actual reader of fantasy, what all us readers of fantasy expect when we open a fantasy novel or watch a movie.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ They literally write:<br /><i>When they open a fantasy novel or go to see a fantasy movie, they expect to find a chosen hero, whose high and lonely destiny is to defeat the villain, whatever the personal sacrifice to himself. We wanted to tell a story about a protagonist who had all the markers of a hero: tragedy and secrets in his past, magical power.</i> No, we do not expect that, that is simply what authors like these two think we expect when we read fantasy. There are several good books who do not follow this tope. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />But let's take this one step at a time and start with the characters that are introduced here:<br /><br /><b>Alastair</b><br />Great name for a mage; not cliché sounding at all, right? In the prologue we see him as a man who cares about his wife and son, who is willing to overcome great obstacles<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[, like climbing up a glacier, <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> to see them safe. All fine and good, but we also see him as a mage of incredibly stupidity and incompetence, which the book seems to sell as competence apparently. The way magic is used respectively not used, as well as what is described as what he notices (plus what a normal person would notice is not even described here) and how he often acts, shows him as a person whom I would not trust with a package of fresh eggs.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />When he climbs up that glacier he shapes handholds and footholds instead of a latter or a moving platform. Surely the first would not have been more energy consuming than all those holds right? And if this weren't enough, apparently to get close to the glacier he flew on an air elemental and then abandoned it because <i>the Enemy’s control of elementals was unpredictable and strong</i>, then why could he fly with it before? How far is the reach of "the Enemy"? Why not take the risk, since apparently time is of the essence, and fly to the entry of the cave and just in case have something to save you when you fall? I mean for flying on that thing and climbing up a glacier he surely would have needed that right? Everyone with half a brain would.<br />And speaking of having only half a brain: who in such a situation just drops his/her googles (for protection against the snow and wind) on the ground instead of stuffing them in a pocket? I would do it on reflex so ingrained would it be in my brain. What will this guy do if he has to flee quickly into the storm outside? Which might very well happen.<br />And further more if he has only <i>dregs of his power</i> (I assume magic) left why does he use it to steady himself? If he is that weak wouldn't it be smarter to conserve magic and recover along the way? But hey a guy knowing he goes into a cave and not providing illumination for himself is just that stupid, albeit he doesn't seem to need them since even deep inside the cave the ice still provides enough illumination to see so many details (including a lot of faces he knows… are there so little mages in this world?). And no, nothing indicates that it's magic light.<br />And if this stupidity and weirdness weren't enough, the guy seems to have no paternal instincts since he just lets the blanket his infant son was wrapped in fall to the side and later wrapping him <i>tightly in the blanket</i>, which is a stupid idea for two reasons:<br />1) A simple blanket tightly to the body is not a good protection against the freezing cold.<br />2) The son has obviously, he even notices it, a twisted leg due to the fight in the cave, so again wrapping the blanket tightly around him, especially without any cushioning or stabilizing for the leg is just plain torture for the infant.<br />Why he thanks his wife for protecting their son, since he addresses her corpse, although the scene suggests that his brother-in-law (who is names Declan) did it, is beyond me. Maybe the authors meant that he addresses both, but then the writing is really bad. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />But don't worry in the first chapter he apparently hates magic (it's worse than stealing for him) and is no mage anymore, for some reason. But even if it turns out it wasn't him, I don't care, at all!<br />Because that second version is not any smarter either, and keep in mind its 11-12 years later. Plus his opinion of people is quite snobbish<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[as he simply states that <i>people aren’t too smart either</i> after his son caused a panic with some mice (we will get to that idiocy, promise) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. His antipathy towards magic makes me really wonder why he lets his son participate in the mage test to begin with instead of somehow shielding him from it. Nope he rather tells his son dark stories (plus actually gives him info about magic<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[: <i>Mages draw on the elements — earth, air, water, fire, and even the void, which is the source of the most powerful and terrible magic of all, chaos magic.</i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>) about where the mages would take his son. Idiot!<br />Also since he wears a tweet jacket even in the heat of the day and people expect a British accent and that he looks <i>like Sherlock Holmes in the old BBC show</i>, I do wonder whether he isn't a rip-off of Giles from Buffy.<br /><br /><br /><b>Callum Hunt</b><br />Our special boy, who from one second to the next is suddenly called "Call" with no explanation, of the book apparently<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[, so special in fact that as an infant he was still alive in the glacier cave among the frozen stiff bodies (a <i>woven blanket</i> would not be enough protection) and his tiny body even had no problem being pressed down by the outflung arm of his uncle's corpse <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. And of course, just in line with the pretentious comment by the authors Callum is dark and sarcastic you see.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ He is <i>famous for driving off substitute teachers with sarcastic remarks, he also specialized in annoying principals, hall monitors, and lunch ladies. Guidance counselors, who always started out wanting to help him (the poor boy’s mother had died, after all) wound up hoping he’d never darken the door of their offices again. There was nothing more embarrassing than not being able to come up with a snappy comeback to an angry twelve-year-old.</i> Of course counselors would not be able to deal with him. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> So he is snappy, sarcastic, makes others hate him, has black hair (maybe the front boy on the cover wasn't him), a perpetual scowl and <i>suspicious gray eyes</i>… hm, where have I heard that before?<br />He is also so special that he <i>lurks outside the windows of the comic book store, the arcade and video game store</i> instead of inside and even the mayor knows him. And I guess some stuff he did is supposed to show he has a good heart.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Like that he had rescued a naked mole rat destined to be fed to a boa constrictor in the pet shop (these things are way too expensive to use as snake fodder unless they are close to death), well it's apparently fine to have the boa starve. Plus he <i>also released all the white mice who would have been next on the snake’s dinner menu.</i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. But even then the authors represent someone either incredibly stupid, arrogant or both, even for a 12 year old.<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ After all what other sort of person would think <i>As though a couple of bruises were the most awful thing that could happen to someone. As though his leg was going to get worse.</i> You see technically it could; especially when after years of therapy and surgery <i>he still walked with a sliding limp, like he was trying to get his footing on a boat that was rolling from side to side.</i> Wow that therapy must have sucked. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> He even gets into school fights regularly and to make us sympathetic to him we get told that <i>he was used to getting picked on; other kids thought his leg made him an easy target. Usually, he was able to convince them he wasn’t.</i> Why he would then still get picked on I have no idea apart from his behavior pissing people off constantly, so apparently it's his own fault… again, that sounds so familiar doesn't it?<br />But don't worry, he always seems to come out fine<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[, e.g. he had only a split lip from one time despite being punched in the face, possibly more than once <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Not that this keeps him from being "sarcastic" in any way. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[His response shortly before blacking out was <i> Sorry for being awesome, losers</i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>So even when it's better to shut up he keeps insulting people… seriously, that just seems to familiar doesn't it?<br />And in the end he is so special that his accidental use of magic<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[, creating a fissure that <i>ran from the swings all the way to the shed wall, splitting the small building in half,</i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> is something he finds the most awesome thing that had ever happened but a few lines of his father cause him nightmares about being trapped underground and running away from a <i>monster made of smoke whose eyes swirled with a thousand different evil colors</i>, which might only be a lame attempt at foreshadowing.<br />And I think I finally got what is so familiar about this character. Are you ready?<br />It is the same male main character Cassandra Clare wrote over and over again up to this point.<br /><br /><br /><b>Sarah</b><br />She is Callum's mother<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[, who is dead at the start already, <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, has a special weapon, with a name that sounds to me like a plant fertilizer we sell in Germany, and no real purpose apparently. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Apart from scratching the message <i>Kill the child</i> into the ice while dying instead of doing something to protect her still living son from the cold, or am I supposed to believe the infant never cried while she did this and so she didn't knew he was alive? And if the message referred to Callum I can only say that in the time she scratched that into the ice she would have had time to kill him, wounded and dying or not. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>Kylie</b><br />A blond girl of Callum's age group that might have magic, but comes from a non-magic family. No comment!<br /><br /><b>The Enemy of Death</b><br />Yeah that is really his name and the description we get of him… Let's say it this way, if one of the two authors already has a bad reputation for ripping off it is not very smart to have a description of a villain that practically screams Voldemort rip-off. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Read this: <i>At first, it had seemed impossible — one young man, even one of the Makaris, planning to conquer death itself. But as the Enemy increased in power, and his army of the Chaos-ridden grew, the threat had become inescapably dire . . . culminating in this pitiless slaughter of the most helpless, the most innocent.</i> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br /><u>And of course lets come to the downright other weirdness and stupidity of the book:</u><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />1) There is snow everywhere and it’s windy and yet the <i>tendrils of his black hair</i> are only damp and are just now getting frozen? If it's that cold they should have been frozen already.<br />2) He apparently climbs a glazier vertically up, so how does wind blow into his face? Since when does wind blow down a straight glacier wall?<br />3) Is the book suggesting that ice is the worst of the elements to tame?<br />4) Why do mages too old or young to fight, injured and sick, hide in a cave inside a cave high in a glacier? Seems like a bad hiding place and difficult to get to.<br />5) <i>The cave walls were white ice, pocked with red and brown where blood had splattered and melted in patches.</i> Of course its white, it’s in a glacier, and what on earth did melt there? Blood? Ice? And since when does blood turn brown? Are these not humans?<br />6) If the fallen mage was burned beyond recognition, how can Alastair tell the sex? Also why was there a leather wristband still intact and visible?<br />7) They have been at war for a decade and yet there were no signs of missing guards at the cave entrance? Or maybe some security spells? Seriously, how does magic work and is used here?<br />8) If all illumination is an <i>eerie phosphorescent glow</i> and the <i>only light he had to see by</i>, how strong is it to reach that deep into the cave? Ice doesn't reflect that much.<br />9) As there are bodies of Chaos-ridden are among the dead, shouldn't there be signa of magic use on the walls and all? The book even says <i>Though the mages had been unprepared, they must have put up quite a fight to have slain so many of the Enemy’s forces.</i><br />10) The authors don't need to tell us twice that Alastair heard a cry, especially not in two consecutive sentences.<br />11) The cave is made out of ice and only some bodies are frozen stiff as statues? How long have they been lying there?<br />12) There is no mentioning of any medical equipment or beds in the cave.<br />13) Why is earth magic used for healing? Wouldn't water make more sense?<br />14) What does <i>Tears thick in his throat</i> mean?<br />15) How poor are the people in this North Carolina town when several cars <i>still bore dings from some of</i> Callum's earlier attempts at skateboarding? <br />16) Snakes don't eat as much as is suggested here, plus how small is that boa if it's fed with mice?<br />17) It's impossible to have onlookers at a parade run away from mice who escaped from a pet shop. You would need hundreds of mice and chase them directly towards the direction of the crowd and that alone would be quite an achievement and requires a lot of preparation since mice do not act that way.<br />18) I guess that woman called Mrs. Kemal is some tokenism, the name is Turk and I wonder how many Turks are there in North Carolina.<br />19) If they can do healing magic, why should <i>the worse off you look, the likelier they are to reject you</i> have any significance in the mage test? And isn't there a war still going on or did go on? Surely they can't afford to be too picky.<br />20) A long fissure like the one described would alert a lot of people due to the tremors it would create.<br />21) If mages often go to war, how many are there? Plus that not only sounds like The Mortal Instruments but also makes the probability to fail any mage test seem very low.<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br />So basically I read 15 pages, in this version 5 % of the book, and I already got pretentious comments by the authors, immense stupidity and incompentence, weird descriptions with lots of holes, a magic system that makes no sense, animal (including human) behavior that makes no sense, several things that scream rip-off and copying and a main male character who is a 12 year old asshole I couldn't care less about. So yeah, not going to read the rest!!!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9702526566029655651" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

YA Fantasy book about a boy who goes to a magic school that has their students wear silver bracelets with stones to indicate the kinds of magic they have mastered. [s]</a>

          <a title="Fantasy Buddy Reads" class="actionLink" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/21109832-the-iron-trial-feb-8-2020">Fantasy Buddy Reads:</a>
        </b>
      
			<a class="actionLink" href="/topic/show/21109832-the-iron-trial-feb-8-2020">The Iron Trial [Feb 8, 2020]</a>
		</td>
		<td class="nobreak">

	<tr>
		<td>
		    <b>
          <a title="Challenge Corner" class="actionLink" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/20928535-the-iron-trial-magisterium-1-by-holly-black---starting-december-9th">Challenge Corner:</a>

			<a class="actionLink" href="/topic/show/20928535-the-iron-trial-magisterium-1-by-holly-black---starting-december-9th">The Iron Trial (Magisterium #1), by Holly Black - starting December 9th, 2019</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('A3TVY4AUvgEH9lLmz7SMdHqrbkHgPVNdEHmSGGJNyu3dV4peIm1j9BCB5HJYszMXZ7iNGTmV9MWrTXps+xyUEg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('0c/patc3YMLeMKXFAmrLDO7040TyHXNc/D62jExCwjEP7LZXdU69N8lHE1GVbXRv8+cAHCu11MRHCl741ROczg==')})
  }
